</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Immune system response to a substance that most people tolerate well</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For the journal, see <a href="/wiki/Allergy_(journal)" title="Allergy (journal)">Allergy (journal)</a>. For the (G)I-dle song, see <a href="/wiki/Allergy_(song)" title="Allergy (song)">Allergy (song)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>

rhinitis">Hay fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_allergy" title="Food allergy">food allergies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atopic_dermatitis" title="Atopic dermatitis">atopic dermatitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allergic_asthma" class="mw-redirect" title="Allergic asthma">allergic asthma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Types&#95;1-0" href="/wiki/Cause_(medicine)" title="Cause (medicine)">Causes</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">Genetic</a> and environmental factors<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kay2000&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kay2000-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="mw-redirect" title="Skin prick test">skin prick test</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blood_test" title="Blood test">blood test</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2012pdf&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2012pdf-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Differential_diagnosis" title="Differential diagnosis">Differential diagnosis</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Food_intolerances" class="mw-redirect" title="Food intolerances">Food intolerances</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_poisoning" class="mw-redirect" title="Food poisoning">food poisoning</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bah2012&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bah2012-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Prevention</th><td class="infobox-data">Early exposure to potential allergens<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sic2014&#95;5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sic2014-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Treatment</th><td class="infobox-data">Avoiding known allergens, medications, <a href="/wiki/Allergen_immunotherapy" title="Allergen immunotherapy">allergen immunotherapy</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Imm&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><a href="/wiki/Medication" title="Medication">Medication</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">Steroids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Histamine_antagonist" class="mw-redirect" title="Histamine antagonist">antihistamines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine_(medication)" title="Epinephrine (medication)">epinephrine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mast_cell_stabilizer" title="Mast cell stabilizer">mast cell stabilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antileukotriene" title="Antileukotriene">antileukotrienes</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Imm&#95;6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Review09&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Review09-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Frequency</th><td class="infobox-data">Common<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Epi&#95;10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Epi-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236303919">@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox-has-images-with-white-backgrounds img{background:white}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox-has-images-with-white-backgrounds img{background:white}}</style>
<p>An <b>allergy</b> is an exaggerated <a href="/wiki/Immune_response" title="Immune response">immune response</a> where the body mistakenly identifies an ordinarily harmless <a href="/wiki/Allergen" title="Allergen">allergen</a> as a threat.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Con2007&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Con2007-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Allergic reactions give rise to <b>allergic diseases</b> such as <a href="/wiki/Allergic_rhinitis" title="Allergic rhinitis">hay fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allergic_conjunctivitis" title="Allergic conjunctivitis">allergic conjunctivitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Allergic_asthma" class="mw-redirect" title="Allergic asthma">allergic asthma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atopic_dermatitis" title="Atopic dermatitis">atopic dermatitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_allergy" title="Food allergy">food allergies</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Types&#95;1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Types-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Symptoms of allergic diseases may include <a href="/wiki/Allergic_conjunctivitis" title="Allergic conjunctivitis">red eyes</a>, an itchy <a href="/wiki/Rash" title="Rash">rash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sneeze" title="Sneeze">sneezing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coughing" class="mw-redirect" title="Coughing">coughing</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Rhinorrhea" title="Rhinorrhea">runny nose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shortness_of_breath" title="Shortness of breath">shortness of breath</a>, or swelling.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Sym&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Sym-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2012pdf&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2012pdf-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bah2012&#95;4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bah2012-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Common <a href="/wiki/Allergen" title="Allergen">allergens</a> include <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>, certain foods, metals, insect stings, medications, and materials, such as <a href="/wiki/Latex_allergy" title="Latex allergy">latex</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Con2007&#95;11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Con2007-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kay2000&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kay2000-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The development of allergies is due to genetic and environmental factors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kay2000&#95;2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kay2000-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The mechanism of allergic reactions involves <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_E_antibodies" class="mw-redirect" title="Immunoglobulin E antibodies">immunoglobulin E antibodies</a> (IgE) binding to an allergen and then to <a href="/wiki/Fc%CE%B5RI" class="mw-redirect" title="FcεRI">a receptor</a> on <a href="/wiki/Mast_cell" title="Mast cell">mast cells</a> or <a href="/wiki/Basophil" title="Basophil">basophils</a>, where they trigger the release of inflammatory chemicals such as <a href="/wiki/Histamine" title="Histamine">histamine</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Diagnosis is typically based on a person's <a href="/wiki/Medical_history" title="Medical history">medical history</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2012pdf&#95;3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2012pdf-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Further testing of the <a href="/wiki/Skin_prick_test" class="mw-redirect" title="Skin prick test">skin</a> or blood may be useful in certain cases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2012pdf&#95;3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2012pdf-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Positive tests, however, may not necessarily mean there is a significant allergy to the substance in question.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cox2008&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cox2008-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Early exposure of children to potential allergens may be protective.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sic2014&#95;5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sic2014-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Treatments for allergies include avoidance of known allergens and the use of medications such as <a href="/wiki/Corticosteroid" title="Corticosteroid">steroids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Histamine_antagonist" class="mw-redirect" title="Histamine antagonist">antihistamines</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Imm&#95;6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In severe reactions, injectable <a href="/wiki/Adrenaline" title="Adrenaline">adrenaline</a> (epinephrine) is recommended.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Review09&#95;7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Review09-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Allergen_immunotherapy" title="Allergen immunotherapy">Allergen immunotherapy</a>, which gradually exposes people to larger and larger amounts of allergen, is useful for some types of allergies such as hay fever and reactions to insect bites.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Imm&#95;6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Its use in food allergies is unclear.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Imm&#95;6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Allergies are common.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Epi&#95;10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Epi-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the developed world, about 20% of people are affected by allergic rhinitis,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NEJM2015&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM2015-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> food allergy affects 10% of adults and 8% of children,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and about 20% have or have had atopic dermatitis at some point in time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Depending on the country, about 1–18% of people have asthma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-GINA2015p2&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GINA2015p2-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-GINA2011p2&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GINA2011p2-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Anaphylaxis occurs in between 0.05–2% of people.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Rates of many allergic diseases appear to be increasing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Review09&#95;7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Review09-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The word "allergy" was first used by <a href="/wiki/Clemens_von_Pirquet" title="Clemens von Pirquet">Clemens von Pirquet</a> in 1906.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kay2000&#95;2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kay2000-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Signs and symptoms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="width:40%; float:right; font-size:90%; margin-left:15px">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Affected organ</th>
<th>Common signs and symptoms
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td>Nose</td>
<td>Swelling of the nasal <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucosa</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rhinitis#Allergic" title="Rhinitis">allergic rhinitis</a>) runny nose, <a href="/wiki/Sneezing" class="mw-redirect" title="Sneezing">sneezing</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Paranasal_sinus" class="mw-redirect" title="Paranasal sinus">Sinuses</a></td>
<td>Allergic <a href="/wiki/Sinusitis" title="Sinusitis">sinusitis</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Human_eye" title="Human eye">Eyes</a></td>
<td>Redness and <a href="/wiki/Itch" title="Itch">itching</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Conjunctiva" title="Conjunctiva">conjunctiva</a> (allergic conjunctivitis, watery)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Airway" class="mw-redirect" title="Airway">Airways</a></td>
<td>Sneezing, coughing, <a href="/wiki/Bronchoconstriction" title="Bronchoconstriction">bronchoconstriction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wheeze" title="Wheeze">wheezing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dyspnea" class="mw-redirect" title="Dyspnea">dyspnea</a>, sometimes outright attacks of <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>. In severe cases, the airway constricts due to swelling known as <a href="/wiki/Laryngeal_edema" class="mw-redirect" title="Laryngeal edema">laryngeal edema</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Ears</td>
<td>Feeling of fullness, possibly pain, and impaired hearing due to the lack of <a href="/wiki/Eustachian_tube" title="Eustachian tube">eustachian tube</a> drainage.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Skin</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Rash" title="Rash">Rashes</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Eczema" class="mw-redirect" title="Eczema">eczema</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urticaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Urticaria">hives (urticaria)</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Human_gastrointestinal_tract" class="mw-redirect" title="Human gastrointestinal tract">Gastrointestinal tract</a></td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Abdominal_pain" title="Abdominal pain">Abdominal pain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bloating" title="Bloating">bloating</a>, vomiting, <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Many allergens, such as dust or pollen, are airborne particles. In these cases, symptoms arise in areas exposed to air, such as the eyes, nose, and lungs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Torres-Borrego&#95;5856&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Torres-Borrego_5856-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For instance, allergic rhinitis, also known as hay fever, irritates the nose, sneezing, itching, and redness of the eyes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Conn&#39;s&#95;Current&#95;Therapy&#95;2005&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Conn&#39;s_Current_Therapy_2005-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Inhaled allergens can also lead to increased production of <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shortness_of_breath" title="Shortness of breath">shortness of breath</a>, coughing, and wheezing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-holgate98&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-holgate98-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Allergic rhinitis can simultaneously occur with other allergic responses such as asthma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Aside from these ambient allergens, allergic reactions can result from foods, <a href="/wiki/Insect_sting_allergy" title="Insect sting allergy">insect stings</a>, and reactions to medications like <a href="/wiki/Aspirin" title="Aspirin">aspirin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillin</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Torres-Borrego&#95;5856&#95;26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Torres-Borrego_5856-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Symptoms of food allergy include abdominal pain, <a href="/wiki/Bloating" title="Bloating">bloating</a>, vomiting, <a href="/wiki/Diarrhea" title="Diarrhea">diarrhea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Itch" title="Itch">itchy</a> skin, and <a href="/wiki/Angioedema" title="Angioedema">hives</a>. Food allergies rarely cause <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_tract" title="Respiratory tract">respiratory</a> (asthmatic) reactions, or <a href="/wiki/Rhinitis" title="Rhinitis">rhinitis</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rusznak98&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rusznak98-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>Insect stings, food, <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a>, and certain medicines may produce a systemic allergic response that is also called anaphylaxis; multiple organ systems can be affected, including the <a href="/wiki/Digestive_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Digestive system">digestive system</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiratory system</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Circulatory_system" title="Circulatory system">circulatory system</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Insect&#95;sting&#95;anaphylaxis&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Insect_sting_anaphylaxis-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Penicillin&#95;allergy&#95;skin&#95;testing:&#95;what&#95;do&#95;we&#95;do&#95;now?&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penicillin_allergy_skin_testing:_what_do_we_do_now?-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-tang03&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tang03-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Depending on the severity, anaphylaxis can include skin reactions, <a href="/wiki/Bronchoconstriction" title="Bronchoconstriction">bronchoconstriction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edema" title="Edema">swelling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">low blood pressure</a>, coma, and death. This type of reaction can be triggered suddenly or have a delayed onset. The anaphylactic reaction may seem to subside, but may recur over time.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tang03&#95;34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tang03-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Skin">Skin</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Skin"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Substances that come into contact with the skin, such as <a href="/wiki/Latex" title="Latex">latex</a>, are also common causes of allergic reactions, known as <a href="/wiki/Contact_dermatitis" title="Contact dermatitis">contact dermatitis</a> or eczema.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Natural&#95;rubber&#95;latex&#95;allergy:&#95;a&#95;problem&#95;of&#95;interdisciplinary&#95;concern&#95;in&#95;medicine&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Natural_rubber_latex_allergy:_a_problem_of_interdisciplinary_concern_in_medicine-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Skin allergies frequently cause <a href="/wiki/Rash" title="Rash">rashes</a>, or swelling and inflammation within the skin, in what is known as a "<a href="/wiki/Wheal_response" class="mw-redirect" title="Wheal response">wheal</a> and flare" reaction characteristic of hives and <a href="/wiki/Angioedema" title="Angioedema">angioedema</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Urticaria&#95;and&#95;angioedema:&#95;a&#95;practical&#95;approach&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Urticaria_and_angioedema:_a_practical_approach-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Allergy_test_on_arm.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Allergy_test_on_arm.jpg/250px-Allergy_test_on_arm.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="329" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Allergy_test_on_arm.jpg/500px-Allergy_test_on_arm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2964" data-file-height="3898" /></a><figcaption>A 21-year-old male arms during an allergy skin test. A visible raised, red wheal indicates a positive allergic reaction. The positive control (H) shows a strong reaction, while the negative control (C) shows no visible response.</figcaption></figure>
<p>With insect stings, a large local reaction may occur as an area of skin redness greater than 10&#160;cm in size that can last one to two days.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lud2015&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lud2015-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This reaction may also occur after <a href="/wiki/Immunotherapy" title="Immunotherapy">immunotherapy</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The skin forms an effective barrier to the entry of most allergens, but this barrier cannot withstand everything. For example, an insect sting can breach the barrier and inject <a href="/wiki/Allergen" title="Allergen">allergen</a> into the affected spot. When an allergen enters the epidermis or dermis, it triggers a localized allergic reaction which activates the <a href="/wiki/Mast_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Mast cells">mast cells</a> in the skin resulting in an immediate increase in permeability of blood vessels, leading to fluid leakage and swelling in the affected area.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-New&#95;York:&#95;Garland&#95;Science&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_York:_Garland_Science-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Mast-cell activation also stimulates a skin lesion called the wheal-and-flare reaction.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wheal&#95;and&#95;Flare&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wheal_and_Flare-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Then the release of chemicals from local nerve endings by a nerve <a href="/wiki/Axon_reflex" title="Axon reflex">axon reflex</a> causes the <a href="/wiki/Vasodilation" title="Vasodilation">widening</a> of surrounding cutaneous blood vessels, which causes redness of the surrounding skin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wheal&#95;and&#95;Flare&#95;40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wheal_and_Flare-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>As part of the allergic response, the body has developed a secondary response that,  in some individuals, causes a more widespread and sustained skin swelling.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-New&#95;York:&#95;Garland&#95;Science&#95;39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_York:_Garland_Science-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This usually occurs about 8 hours after the allergen originally comes in contact with the skin. When an allergen is ingested, a dispersed form of wheal-and-flare reaction, known as <a href="/wiki/Urticaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Urticaria">urticaria</a> or hives, will appear when the allergen enters the bloodstream and eventually reaches the skin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-New&#95;York:&#95;Garland&#95;Science&#95;39-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_York:_Garland_Science-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The way the skin reacts to different allergens and allows to test for exiting allergies by injecting a very small amount of an allergen into the skin, which cause skin reaction in the site of injection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-New&#95;York:&#95;Garland&#95;Science&#95;39-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_York:_Garland_Science-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Even though these injections are very small and local, they still pose the risk of causing systemic anaphylaxis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-New&#95;York:&#95;Garland&#95;Science&#95;39-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_York:_Garland_Science-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Cause">Cause</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cause"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Risk factors for allergies can be placed in two broad categories: <a href="/wiki/Host_(biology)" title="Host (biology)">host</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environmental</a> factors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-The&#95;genetic&#95;and&#95;environmental&#95;basis&#95;of&#95;atopic&#95;diseases&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_genetic_and_environmental_basis_of_atopic_diseases-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Host factors include <a href="/wiki/Heredity" title="Heredity">heredity</a>, sex, <a href="/wiki/Race_(classification_of_human_beings)" class="mw-redirect" title="Race (classification of human beings)">race</a>, and age, with heredity being by far the most significant. However, there has been a recent increase in the incidence of allergic disorders that cannot be explained by genetic factors alone. Four major environmental candidates are alterations in exposure to <a href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Infectious disease">infectious diseases</a> during early childhood, <a href="/wiki/Pollution" title="Pollution">environmental pollution</a>, allergen levels, and <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">dietary</a> changes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Janeway&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Dust_mites">Dust mites</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Dust mites"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dust_mite_allergy" title="Dust mite allergy">Dust mite allergy</a></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:House_dust_mites_(5247996458).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/House_dust_mites_%285247996458%29.jpg/330px-House_dust_mites_%285247996458%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="300" height="201" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/House_dust_mites_%285247996458%29.jpg/960px-House_dust_mites_%285247996458%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4876" data-file-height="3264" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/House_dust_mites" class="mw-redirect" title="House dust mites">House dust mites</a> (Dermatophagoides pteronyssinus)</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/House_dust_mite" title="House dust mite">Dust mite</a> allergy, also known as house dust allergy, is a <a href="/wiki/Sensitization_(immunology)" title="Sensitization (immunology)">sensitization</a> and allergic reaction to the droppings of <a href="/wiki/House_dust_mite" title="House dust mite">house dust mites</a>. The allergy is common<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and can trigger allergic reactions such as asthma, <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis" title="Dermatitis">eczema</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Itch" title="Itch">itching</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The mite's gut contains potent <a href="/wiki/Digestive_enzymes" class="mw-redirect" title="Digestive enzymes">digestive enzymes</a> (notably <a href="/wiki/Peptidase_1_(mite)" title="Peptidase 1 (mite)">peptidase 1</a>) that persist in their faeces and are major inducers of allergic reactions such as <a href="/wiki/Wheeze" title="Wheeze">wheezing</a>. The mite's <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeleton</a> can also contribute to allergic reactions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Unlike <a href="/wiki/Scabies" title="Scabies">scabies</a> mites or skin <a href="/wiki/Demodex" title="Demodex">follicle mites</a>, house dust mites do not burrow under the skin and are not parasitic.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-unl&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unl-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Dust mite-proof encasements to <a href="/wiki/Mattress" title="Mattress">mattress</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pillow" title="Pillow">pillow</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Duvet" title="Duvet">duvet</a> prevent chronic contact with allergens.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MedlinePlus&#95;2019&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MedlinePlus_2019-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Foods">Foods</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Foods"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Food_allergy" title="Food allergy">Food allergy</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Summer_Palace_restaurant_menu_back_pages_displaying_vegetarian_options,_rice_dishes,_set_dinners_and_desserts_with_allergy_information.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Summer_Palace_restaurant_menu_back_pages_displaying_vegetarian_options%2C_rice_dishes%2C_set_dinners_and_desserts_with_allergy_information.jpg/250px-Summer_Palace_restaurant_menu_back_pages_displaying_vegetarian_options%2C_rice_dishes%2C_set_dinners_and_desserts_with_allergy_information.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Summer_Palace_restaurant_menu_back_pages_displaying_vegetarian_options%2C_rice_dishes%2C_set_dinners_and_desserts_with_allergy_information.jpg/500px-Summer_Palace_restaurant_menu_back_pages_displaying_vegetarian_options%2C_rice_dishes%2C_set_dinners_and_desserts_with_allergy_information.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="3024" /></a><figcaption>In many countries restaurants are required to display possible food allergens list on the menu.</figcaption></figure>
<p>A wide variety of foods can cause allergic reactions, but 90% of allergic responses to foods are caused by cow's milk, <a href="/wiki/Soy" class="mw-redirect" title="Soy">soy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egg" title="Egg">eggs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">peanuts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tree_nuts" class="mw-redirect" title="Tree nuts">tree nuts</a>, fish, and <a href="/wiki/Shellfish" title="Shellfish">shellfish</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-aafa.org&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aafa.org-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other food allergies, affecting less than 1 person per 10,000 population, may be considered "rare".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Maleki&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maleki-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The most common food allergy in the US population is a sensitivity to <a href="/wiki/Crustacea" class="mw-redirect" title="Crustacea">crustacea</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Maleki&#95;50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maleki-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Peanut_allergies" class="mw-redirect" title="Peanut allergies">peanut allergies</a> are notorious for their severity, peanut allergies are not the most common food allergy in adults or children. Other allergens may trigger severe or life-threatening reactions and are more common when combined with <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-aafa.org&#95;49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aafa.org-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Rates of allergies differ between adults and children. Children can sometimes outgrow peanut allergies. <a href="/wiki/Egg_allergies" class="mw-redirect" title="Egg allergies">Egg allergies</a> affect one to two percent of children but are outgrown by about two-thirds of children by the age of 5.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The sensitivity is usually to proteins in the <a href="/wiki/Egg_white" title="Egg white">white</a>, rather than the <a href="/wiki/Yolk" title="Yolk">yolk</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sicherer&#95;63&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sicherer_63-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Milk_allergy" title="Milk allergy">Milk-protein allergies</a>—distinct from <a href="/wiki/Lactose_intolerance" title="Lactose intolerance">lactose intolerance</a>—are most common in children.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Approximately 60% of milk-protein reactions are <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_E" title="Immunoglobulin E">immunoglobulin E</a>–mediated, with the remaining usually attributable to <a href="/wiki/Proctocolitis" title="Proctocolitis">inflammation of the colon</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some people are unable to tolerate milk from goats or sheep as well as from cows, and many are also unable to tolerate dairy products such as cheese. Roughly 10% of children with a milk allergy will have a reaction to beef.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Lactose intolerance, a common reaction to milk, is not a form of allergy at all, but due to the absence of an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Digestive_tract" class="mw-redirect" title="Digestive tract">digestive tract</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-m234&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m234-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Those with <a href="/wiki/Tree_nut_allergies" class="mw-redirect" title="Tree nut allergies">tree nut allergies</a> may be allergic to one or many <a href="/wiki/Tree_nuts" class="mw-redirect" title="Tree nuts">tree nuts</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Pecan" title="Pecan">pecans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pistachios" class="mw-redirect" title="Pistachios">pistachios</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Walnut" title="Walnut">walnuts</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sicherer&#95;63&#95;52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sicherer_63-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In addition, <a href="/wiki/Seeds" class="mw-redirect" title="Seeds">seeds</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Sesame_seeds" class="mw-redirect" title="Sesame seeds">sesame seeds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Poppy_seed" title="Poppy seed">poppy seeds</a>, contain oils in which protein is present, which may elicit an allergic reaction.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sicherer&#95;63&#95;52-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sicherer_63-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Allergens can be transferred from one food to another through <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a>; however, genetic modification can also remove allergens. Little research has been done on the natural variation of allergen concentrations in unmodified crops.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Latex">Latex</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Latex"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Latex_mask_eg.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Latex_mask_eg.jpg/250px-Latex_mask_eg.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="284" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/45/Latex_mask_eg.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="568" /></a><figcaption>Latex masks is one of the latex-containing products that can cause allergy.</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Latex" title="Latex">Latex</a> can trigger an IgE-mediated cutaneous, respiratory, and systemic reaction.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The prevalence of latex allergy in the general population is believed to be less than one percent. In a hospital study, 1 in 800 surgical patients (0.125 percent) reported latex sensitivity, although the sensitivity among healthcare workers is higher, between seven and ten percent.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Researchers attribute this higher level to the exposure of healthcare workers to areas with significant airborne latex allergens, such as operating rooms, intensive-care units, and dental suites. These latex-rich environments may sensitize healthcare workers who regularly inhale allergenic proteins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sussman&#95;61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sussman-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The most prevalent response to latex is allergic contact dermatitis, a delayed hypersensitivity reaction that manifests as dry, crusted lesions. This reaction usually lasts 48–96 hours. Sweating or rubbing the area under the glove aggravates the lesions, possibly leading to ulcerations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sussman&#95;61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sussman-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anaphylactic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anaphylactic">Anaphylactic</a> reactions occur most often in sensitive patients who have been exposed to a surgeon's latex gloves during abdominal surgery, but other <a href="/wiki/Mucous_membrane" title="Mucous membrane">mucosal</a> exposures, such as dental procedures, can also produce systemic reactions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sussman&#95;61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sussman-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Latex and banana sensitivity may cross-react. Furthermore, those with latex allergy may also have sensitivities to <a href="/wiki/Avocado" title="Avocado">avocado</a>, kiwifruit, and chestnut.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These people often have <a href="/wiki/Perioral" class="mw-redirect" title="Perioral">perioral</a> itching and local <a href="/wiki/Urticaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Urticaria">urticaria</a>. Only occasionally have these food-induced allergies induced systemic responses. Researchers suspect that the cross-reactivity of latex with banana, <a href="/wiki/Avocado" title="Avocado">avocado</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kiwifruit" title="Kiwifruit">kiwifruit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chestnut" title="Chestnut">chestnut</a> occurs because latex proteins are structurally <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">homologous</a> with some other plant proteins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sussman&#95;61-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sussman-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Medications">Medications</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Medications"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Drug_allergy" title="Drug allergy">Drug allergy</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Adverse_drug_reaction" title="Adverse drug reaction">Adverse drug reaction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Drug_eruption" title="Drug eruption">Drug eruption</a></div>
<p>About 10% of people report that they are allergic to <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillin</a>; however, of that 10%, 90% turn out not to be.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Al2015&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Al2015-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Serious allergies only occur in about 0.03%.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Al2015&#95;63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Al2015-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:530424647922ef532def2o.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/530424647922ef532def2o.jpg/250px-530424647922ef532def2o.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="194" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/530424647922ef532def2o.jpg/500px-530424647922ef532def2o.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1129" data-file-height="878" /></a><figcaption>During a sting <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquito</a> injects saliva proteins into the human bloodstream, which can cause allergy.</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Insect_stings">Insect stings</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Insect stings"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Insect_sting_allergy" title="Insect sting allergy">Insect sting allergy</a></div>
<p>One of the main sources of human allergies is insects. Insect bites, stings, ingestion, and inhalation can trigger an insect allergy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-f951&#95;64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-f951-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Toxins_interacting_with_proteins">Toxins interacting with proteins</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Toxins interacting with proteins"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Another non-food protein reaction, <a href="/wiki/Urushiol-induced_contact_dermatitis" title="Urushiol-induced contact dermatitis">urushiol-induced contact dermatitis</a>, originates after contact with <a href="/wiki/Poison_ivy" title="Poison ivy">poison ivy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toxicodendron_pubescens" title="Toxicodendron pubescens">eastern poison oak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toxicodendron_diversilobum" title="Toxicodendron diversilobum">western poison oak</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Poison_sumac" class="mw-redirect" title="Poison sumac">poison sumac</a>. <a href="/wiki/Urushiol" title="Urushiol">Urushiol</a>, which is not itself a protein, acts as a <a href="/wiki/Hapten" title="Hapten">hapten</a> and chemically reacts with, binds to, and changes the shape of <a href="/wiki/Integral_membrane_protein" title="Integral membrane protein">integral membrane proteins</a> on exposed skin cells. The immune system does not recognize the affected cells as normal parts of the body, causing a <a href="/wiki/T-cell" class="mw-redirect" title="T-cell">T-cell</a>-mediated <a href="/wiki/Immune_response" title="Immune response">immune response</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Of these poisonous plants, sumac is the most virulent.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The resulting dermatological response to the reaction between urushiol and membrane proteins includes redness, swelling, <a href="/wiki/Papule" title="Papule">papules</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vesicle_(dermatology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Vesicle (dermatology)">vesicles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blister" title="Blister">blisters</a>, and streaking.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dermatlas&#95;68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dermatlas-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Estimates vary on the fraction of the population that will have an immune system response. Approximately 25% of the population will have a strong allergic response to urushiol. In general, approximately 80–90% of adults will develop a rash if they are exposed to 0.0050&#160;mg (7.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>−5</sup>&#160;gr) of purified <a href="/wiki/Urushiol" title="Urushiol">urushiol</a>. Some people are so sensitive that a molecular trace on the skin can initiate an allergic reaction.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Genetics">Genetics</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Genetics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Mauch_Twins.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mauch_Twins.jpg/250px-Mauch_Twins.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="296" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mauch_Twins.jpg/500px-Mauch_Twins.jpg 2x" data-file-width="523" data-file-height="620" /></a><figcaption>Identical twins are similar not only visually but in many reactions to the diseases including allergic reactions.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Allergic diseases are strongly <a href="/wiki/Family" title="Family">familial</a>; <a href="/wiki/Twin#Monozygotic_twins" title="Twin">identical twins</a> are likely to have the same allergic diseases about 70% of the time; the same allergy occurs about 40% of the time in <a href="/wiki/Twin#Dizygotic_twins" title="Twin">non-identical twins</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Allergy&#95;70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allergy-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Allergic parents are more likely to have allergic children<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DeSwert&#95;71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeSwert-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and those children's allergies are likely to be more severe than those in children of non-allergic parents. Some allergies, however, are not consistent along <a href="/wiki/Genealogy" title="Genealogy">genealogies</a>; parents who are allergic to peanuts may have children who are allergic to <a href="/wiki/Ragweed" title="Ragweed">ragweed</a>. The likelihood of developing allergies is <a href="/wiki/Heredity" title="Heredity">inherited</a> and related to an irregularity in the immune system, but the specific <a href="/wiki/Allergen" title="Allergen">allergen</a> is not.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DeSwert&#95;71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeSwert-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The risk of allergic <a href="/wiki/Sensitization_(immunology)" title="Sensitization (immunology)">sensitization</a> and the development of allergies varies with age, with young children most at risk.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Croner&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Croner-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Several studies have shown that IgE levels are highest in childhood and fall rapidly between the ages of 10 and 30 years.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Croner&#95;72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Croner-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The peak prevalence of hay fever is highest in children and young adults, and the incidence of asthma is highest in children under 10.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic group">Ethnicity</a> may play a role in some allergies; however, racial factors have been difficult to separate from environmental influences and changes due to <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">migration</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DeSwert&#95;71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeSwert-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has been suggested that different <a href="/wiki/Locus_(genetics)" title="Locus (genetics)">genetic loci</a> are responsible for asthma, to be specific, in people of <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_race" title="Caucasian race">European</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hispanic" title="Hispanic">Hispanic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asian_people" title="Asian people">Asian</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_of_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic groups of Africa">African</a> origins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-African&#95;Americans&#95;with&#95;asthma:&#95;genetic&#95;insights&#95;74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-African_Americans_with_asthma:_genetic_insights-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Researchers have worked to characterize genes involved in inflammation and the maintenance of mucosal integrity. The identified genes associated with allergic disease severity, progression, and development primarily function in four areas: regulating inflammatory responses (<a href="/wiki/IFN-%CE%B1" class="mw-redirect" title="IFN-α">IFN-α</a>, TLR-1, <a href="/wiki/Interleukin_13" title="Interleukin 13">IL-13</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interleukin_4" title="Interleukin 4">IL-4</a>, IL-5, HLA-G, iNOS), maintaining vascular endothelium and mucosal lining (FLG, PLAUR, CTNNA3, PDCH1, COL29A1), mediating immune cell function (PHF11, H1R, HDC, TSLP, STAT6, RERE, PPP2R3C), and influencing susceptibility to allergic sensitization (e.g., ORMDL3, CHI3L1).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Frontiers&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frontiers-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Multiple studies have investigated the genetic profiles of individuals with predispositions to and experiences of allergic diseases, revealing a complex polygenic architecture. Specific genetic loci, such as MIIP, CXCR4, SCML4, CYP1B1, ICOS, and LINC00824, have been directly associated with allergic disorders.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Frontiers&#95;75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frontiers-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Additionally, some loci show pleiotropic effects, linking them to both autoimmune and allergic conditions, including PRDM2, G3BP1, HBS1L, and POU2AF1.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Frontiers&#95;75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frontiers-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These genes engage in shared inflammatory pathways across various epithelial tissues—such as the skin, esophagus, vagina, and lung—highlighting common genetic factors that contribute to the pathogenesis of asthma and other allergic diseases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Frontiers&#95;75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frontiers-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In atopic patients, transcriptome studies have identified IL-13-related pathways as key for eosinophilic airway inflammation and remodeling. That causes the body to experience the type of airflow restriction of allergic asthma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Frontiers&#95;75-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frontiers-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Expression of genes was quite variable: genes associated with inflammation were found almost exclusively in superficial airways, while genes related to airway remodeling were mainly present in endobronchial biopsy specimens.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Frontiers&#95;75-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frontiers-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This enhanced gene profile was similar across multiple sample sizes – nasal brushing, sputum, endobronchial brushing – demonstrating the importance of eosinophilic inflammation, mast cell degranulation and group 3 innate lymphoid cells in severe adult-onset asthma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Frontiers&#95;75-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frontiers-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> IL-13 is an immunoregulatory cytokine that is made mostly by activated T-helper 2 (Th2) cells.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-U.S.&#95;National&#95;Library&#95;of&#95;Medicine&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._National_Library_of_Medicine-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is an important cytokine for many steps in B-cell maturation and differentiation, since it increases CD23 and MHC class II molecules, and aids in B-cell isotype switching to IgE.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Frontiers&#95;75-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frontiers-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-U.S.&#95;National&#95;Library&#95;of&#95;Medicine&#95;76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._National_Library_of_Medicine-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> IL-13 also suppresses macrophage function by reducing the release of pro-inflammatory cytokines and chemokines.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-U.S.&#95;National&#95;Library&#95;of&#95;Medicine&#95;76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._National_Library_of_Medicine-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The more striking thing is that IL-13 is the prime mover in allergen-induced asthma via pathways that are independent of IgE and eosinophils.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-U.S.&#95;National&#95;Library&#95;of&#95;Medicine&#95;76-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-U.S._National_Library_of_Medicine-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Hygiene_hypothesis">Hygiene hypothesis</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Hygiene hypothesis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hygiene_hypothesis" title="Hygiene hypothesis">Hygiene hypothesis</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Klimt_hygeia.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Klimt_hygeia.jpg/250px-Klimt_hygeia.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="372" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Klimt_hygeia.jpg/500px-Klimt_hygeia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="614" data-file-height="914" /></a><figcaption>Greek goddess of cleanliness <a href="/wiki/Hygieia" title="Hygieia">Hygieia</a> by <a href="/wiki/Gustav_Klimt" title="Gustav Klimt">Gustav Klimt</a>  (1900 - 1907)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Allergic diseases are caused by inappropriate immunological responses to harmless <a href="/wiki/Antigens" class="mw-redirect" title="Antigens">antigens</a> driven by a <a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="T helper cell">TH2</a>-mediated immune response. Many bacteria and viruses elicit a <a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="T helper cell">TH1</a>-mediated immune response, which down-regulates TH2 responses. The first proposed mechanism of action of the hygiene hypothesis was that insufficient stimulation of the TH1 arm of the immune system leads to an overactive TH2 arm, which in turn leads to allergic disease.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In other words, individuals living in too sterile an environment are not exposed to enough pathogens to keep the immune system busy. Since our bodies evolved to deal with a certain level of such pathogens, when they are not exposed to this level, the immune system will attack harmless antigens. Thus, normally benign microbial objects—like pollen—will trigger an immune response.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The hygiene hypothesis was developed to explain the observation that <a href="/wiki/Hay_fever" class="mw-redirect" title="Hay fever">hay fever</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eczema" class="mw-redirect" title="Eczema">eczema</a>, both allergic diseases, were less common in children from larger families, which were, it is presumed, exposed to more infectious agents through their siblings, than in children from families with only one child.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-z256&#95;80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-z256-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is used to explain the increase in allergic diseases that have been seen since industrialization, and the higher incidence of allergic diseases in more developed countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-e520&#95;81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e520-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The hygiene hypothesis has now expanded to include exposure to symbiotic bacteria and parasites as important modulators of immune system development, along with infectious agents.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-g732&#95;82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-g732-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Epidemiological data support the hygiene hypothesis. Studies have shown that various immunological and autoimmune diseases are much less common in the developing world than in the industrialized world, and that immigrants to the industrialized world from the developing world increasingly develop immunological disorders in relation to the length of time since arrival in the industrialized world.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid12910582&#95;83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12910582-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Longitudinal studies in the third world demonstrate an increase in immunological disorders as a country grows more affluent and, it is presumed, cleaner.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid17326711&#95;84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17326711-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The use of antibiotics in the first year of life has been linked to asthma and other allergic diseases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Antibiotic&#95;exposure&#95;during&#95;infancy&#95;85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Antibiotic_exposure_during_infancy-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The use of antibacterial cleaning products has also been associated with a higher incidence of asthma, as has birth by <a href="/wiki/Caesarean_section" title="Caesarean section">caesarean section</a> rather than vaginal birth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-A&#95;meta-analysis&#95;of&#95;the&#95;association&#95;between&#95;Caesarean&#95;section&#95;and&#95;childhood&#95;asthma&#95;86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A_meta-analysis_of_the_association_between_Caesarean_section_and_childhood_asthma-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-The&#95;use&#95;of&#95;household&#95;cleaning&#95;sprays&#95;and&#95;adult&#95;asthma:&#95;an&#95;international&#95;longitudinal&#95;study&#95;87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_use_of_household_cleaning_sprays_and_adult_asthma:_an_international_longitudinal_study-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Stress">Stress</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Stress"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Chronic <a href="/wiki/Stress_(psychological)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stress (psychological)">stress</a> can aggravate allergic conditions. This has been attributed to a T helper 2 (TH2)-predominant response driven by suppression of <a href="/wiki/Interleukin_12" title="Interleukin 12">interleukin 12</a> by both the <a href="/wiki/Autonomic_nervous_system" title="Autonomic nervous system">autonomic nervous system</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamic%E2%80%93pituitary%E2%80%93adrenal_axis" title="Hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal axis">hypothalamic–pituitary–adrenal axis</a>. Stress management in highly susceptible individuals may improve symptoms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other_environmental_factors">Other environmental factors</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Other environmental factors"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Allergic diseases are more common in industrialized countries than in countries that are more traditional or agricultural, and there is a higher rate of allergic disease in urban populations versus rural populations, although these differences are becoming less defined.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-cooper04&#95;89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cooper04-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Historically, the trees planted in urban areas were predominantly male to prevent litter from seeds and fruits, but the high ratio of male trees causes high pollen counts, a phenomenon that horticulturist Tom Ogren has called "<a href="/wiki/Botanical_sexism" title="Botanical sexism">botanical sexism</a>".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Alterations in exposure to <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> is another plausible explanation, at present, for the increase in <a href="/wiki/Atopy" title="Atopy">atopic allergy</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Janeway&#95;43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Endotoxin exposure reduces release of inflammatory <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Tumor_necrosis_factor_alpha" class="mw-redirect" title="Tumor necrosis factor alpha">TNF-α</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interferon-gamma" class="mw-redirect" title="Interferon-gamma">IFNγ</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interleukin-10" class="mw-redirect" title="Interleukin-10">interleukin-10</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Interleukin-12" class="mw-redirect" title="Interleukin-12">interleukin-12</a> from white blood cells (<a href="/wiki/Leukocytes" class="mw-redirect" title="Leukocytes">leukocytes</a>) that circulate in the blood.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid12239255&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12239255-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Certain microbe-sensing <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Toll-like_receptor" title="Toll-like receptor">Toll-like receptors</a>, found on the surface of cells in the body, are also thought to be involved in these processes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Epidemiological&#95;and&#95;immunological&#95;evidence&#95;for&#95;the&#95;hygiene&#95;hypothesis&#95;92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Epidemiological_and_immunological_evidence_for_the_hygiene_hypothesis-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Hookworm_LifeCycle.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Hookworm_LifeCycle.gif/500px-Hookworm_LifeCycle.gif" decoding="async" width="400" height="301" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d3/Hookworm_LifeCycle.gif 2x" data-file-width="579" data-file-height="435" /></a><figcaption>Hookworm <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">life cycle</a>. The adults spend all their life in the gut of infected humans, challenging their immune system.</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Parasitic_worm" title="Parasitic worm">Parasitic worms</a> and similar parasites are present in untreated drinking water in developing countries, and were present in the water of developed countries until the routine <a href="/wiki/Water_chlorination" title="Water chlorination">chlorination</a> and purification of drinking water supplies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Parasitic&#95;food-borne&#95;and&#95;water-borne&#95;zoonoses&#95;93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parasitic_food-borne_and_water-borne_zoonoses-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Recent research has shown that some common parasites, such as intestinal worms (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Hookworm" title="Hookworm">hookworms</a>), secrete chemicals into the gut wall (and, hence, the bloodstream) that <a href="/wiki/Immunosuppressant" class="mw-redirect" title="Immunosuppressant">suppress</a> the immune system and prevent the body from attacking the parasite.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Worms&#95;and&#95;allergy&#95;94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worms_and_allergy-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This gives rise to a new slant on the hygiene hypothesis theory—that <a href="/wiki/Co-evolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Co-evolution">co-evolution</a> of humans and parasites has led to an immune system that functions correctly only in the presence of the parasites. Without them, the immune system becomes unbalanced and oversensitive.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Yazdanbakhsh02&#95;95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yazdanbakhsh02-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In particular, research suggests that allergies may coincide with the delayed establishment of <a href="/wiki/Gut_flora" class="mw-redirect" title="Gut flora">gut flora</a> in <a href="/wiki/Infant" title="Infant">infants</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid17382394&#95;96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17382394-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the research to support this theory is conflicting, with some studies performed in China and <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> showing an increase in allergy in people infected with intestinal worms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-cooper04&#95;89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cooper04-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Clinical trials have been initiated to test the effectiveness of <a href="/wiki/Helminthic_therapy" title="Helminthic therapy">helminthic therapy</a> with certain worms in treating some allergies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-falcone05&#95;97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-falcone05-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It may be that the term 'parasite' could turn out to be inappropriate, and in fact a hitherto unsuspected <a href="/wiki/Mutualism_(biology)" title="Mutualism (biology)">symbiosis</a> is at work.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-falcone05&#95;97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-falcone05-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Pathophysiology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Allergy_Pathway.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/The_Allergy_Pathway.jpg/250px-The_Allergy_Pathway.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="159" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/The_Allergy_Pathway.jpg/500px-The_Allergy_Pathway.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2122" data-file-height="1348" /></a><figcaption>A summary diagram that explains how allergy develops</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Tissues_Affected_In_Allergic_Inflammation.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tissues_Affected_In_Allergic_Inflammation.jpg/250px-Tissues_Affected_In_Allergic_Inflammation.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="196" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tissues_Affected_In_Allergic_Inflammation.jpg/500px-Tissues_Affected_In_Allergic_Inflammation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2037" data-file-height="1596" /></a><figcaption>Tissues affected in <a href="/wiki/Allergic_inflammation" title="Allergic inflammation">allergic inflammation</a></figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Acute_response">Acute response</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Acute response"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Allergy_degranulation_processes_01.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Allergy_degranulation_processes_01.svg/250px-Allergy_degranulation_processes_01.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Allergy_degranulation_processes_01.svg/500px-Allergy_degranulation_processes_01.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption>Degranulation process in allergy. Second exposure to allergen. <b>1</b> – antigen; <b>2</b> – IgE antibody; <b>3</b> – FcεRI receptor; <b>4</b> – preformed mediators (histamine, proteases, chemokines, heparin); <b>5</b> – <a href="/wiki/Granule_(cell_biology)" title="Granule (cell biology)">granules</a>; <b>6</b> – <a href="/wiki/Mast_cell" title="Mast cell">mast cell</a>; <b>7</b> – newly formed mediators (prostaglandins, leukotrienes, thromboxanes, <a href="/wiki/Platelet-activating_factor" title="Platelet-activating factor">PAF</a>).</figcaption></figure>
