<p>In the initial stages of allergy, a type I hypersensitivity reaction against an allergen encountered for the first time and presented by a professional <a href="/wiki/Antigen-presenting_cell" title="Antigen-presenting cell">antigen-presenting cell</a> causes a response in a type of immune cell called a <a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="T helper cell">T<sub>H</sub>2 lymphocyte</a>, a subset of <a href="/wiki/T_cell" title="T cell">T cells</a> that produce a <a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokine</a> called <a href="/wiki/Interleukin-4" class="mw-redirect" title="Interleukin-4">interleukin-4</a> (IL-4). These T<sub>H</sub>2 cells interact with other <a href="/wiki/Lymphocytes" class="mw-redirect" title="Lymphocytes">lymphocytes</a> called <a href="/wiki/B_cell" title="B cell">B cells</a>, whose role is the production of antibodies. Coupled with signals provided by IL-4, this interaction stimulates the B cell to begin production of a large amount of a particular type of antibody known as IgE. Secreted IgE circulates in the blood and binds to an IgE-specific receptor (a kind of <a href="/wiki/Fc_receptor" title="Fc receptor">Fc receptor</a> called <a href="/wiki/Fc%CE%B5RI" class="mw-redirect" title="FcεRI">FcεRI</a>) on the surface of other kinds of immune cells called <a href="/wiki/Mast_cell" title="Mast cell">mast cells</a> and <a href="/wiki/Basophil" title="Basophil">basophils</a>, which are both involved in the acute inflammatory response. At this stage, the IgE-coated cells are sensitized to the allergen.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Janeway&#95;43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>If later exposure to the same allergen occurs, the allergen can bind to IgE molecules held on the surface of the mast cells or basophils. Cross-linking of the IgE and Fc receptors occurs when more than one IgE-receptor complex interacts with the same allergenic molecule and activates the sensitized cell. Activated mast cells and basophils undergo a process called <a href="/wiki/Degranulation" title="Degranulation">degranulation</a>, during which they release <a href="/wiki/Histamine" title="Histamine">histamine</a> and other inflammatory chemical mediators (<a href="/wiki/Cytokine" title="Cytokine">cytokines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interleukin" title="Interleukin">interleukins</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leukotriene" title="Leukotriene">leukotrienes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prostaglandin" title="Prostaglandin">prostaglandins</a>) from their <a href="/wiki/Granule_(cell_biology)" title="Granule (cell biology)">granules</a> into the surrounding tissue causing several systemic effects, such as <a href="/wiki/Vasodilation" title="Vasodilation">vasodilation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucous</a> secretion, <a href="/wiki/Nerve" title="Nerve">nerve</a> stimulation, and <a href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle" title="Smooth muscle">smooth muscle</a> contraction.
</p><p>This results in <a href="/wiki/Rhinorrhea" title="Rhinorrhea">rhinorrhea</a>, itchiness, <a href="/wiki/Shortness_of_breath" title="Shortness of breath">dyspnea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Anaphylaxis" title="Anaphylaxis">anaphylaxis</a>. Depending on the individual, allergen, and mode of introduction, the symptoms can be system-wide (classical anaphylaxis) or localized to specific body systems. Asthma is localized to the respiratory system, and eczema is localized to the <a href="/wiki/Dermis" title="Dermis">dermis</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Janeway&#95;43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Janeway-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Late-phase_response">Late-phase response</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Late-phase response"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>After the chemical mediators of the acute response subside, late-phase responses can often occur. This is due to the migration of other <a href="/wiki/Leukocyte" class="mw-redirect" title="Leukocyte">leukocytes</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Neutrophil" title="Neutrophil">neutrophils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lymphocyte" title="Lymphocyte">lymphocytes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eosinophil" title="Eosinophil">eosinophils</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a> to the initial site. The reaction is usually seen 2–24 hours after the original reaction.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Effector&#95;and&#95;potential&#95;immunoregulatory&#95;roles&#95;of&#95;mast&#95;cells&#95;in&#95;IgE-associated&#95;acquired&#95;immune&#95;responses&#95;98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Effector_and_potential_immunoregulatory_roles_of_mast_cells_in_IgE-associated_acquired_immune_responses-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cytokines from mast cells may play a role in the persistence of long-term effects. Late-phase responses in asthma differ slightly from those seen in other allergic responses. They are still caused by the release of mediators from eosinophils and remain dependent on T<sub>H</sub>2 cell activity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Th2&#95;cytokines&#95;in&#95;the&#95;asthma&#95;late-phase&#95;response&#95;99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Th2_cytokines_in_the_asthma_late-phase_response-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Allergic_contact_dermatitis">Allergic contact dermatitis</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Allergic contact dermatitis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Although <a href="/wiki/Allergic_contact_dermatitis" title="Allergic contact dermatitis">allergic contact dermatitis</a> is termed an "allergic" reaction (which usually refers to type I hypersensitivity), its pathophysiology involves a reaction that more correctly corresponds to a <a href="/wiki/Type_IV_hypersensitivity" title="Type IV hypersensitivity">type IV hypersensitivity</a> reaction.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In type IV hypersensitivity, there is activation of certain types of <a href="/wiki/T_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="T cells">T cells</a> (CD8+) that destroy target cells on contact, as well as activated <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a> that produce <a href="/wiki/Hydrolytic_enzyme" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrolytic enzyme">hydrolytic</a> <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-c364&#95;101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-c364-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Diagnosis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Allergy_testing_machine.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Allergy_testing_machine.jpg/250px-Allergy_testing_machine.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="376" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Allergy_testing_machine.jpg/500px-Allergy_testing_machine.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1993" data-file-height="3000" /></a><figcaption>An allergy testing machine being operated in a diagnostic immunology lab</figcaption></figure>
<p>Effective management of allergic diseases relies on the ability to make an accurate diagnosis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Allergy testing can help confirm or rule out allergies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceB&#95;104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Correct diagnosis, counseling, and avoidance advice based on valid allergy test results reduce the incidence of symptoms and need for medications, and improve quality of life.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To assess the presence of allergen-specific IgE antibodies, two different methods can be used: a skin prick test or an allergy <a href="/wiki/Blood_test" title="Blood test">blood test</a>. Both methods are recommended, and they have similar diagnostic value.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceB&#95;104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Skin prick tests and blood tests are equally cost-effective, and health economic evidence shows that both tests are cost-effective compared with no test.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceA&#95;103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Early and more accurate diagnoses save costs due to reduced consultations, referrals to secondary care, misdiagnosis, and emergency admissions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Allergy undergoes dynamic changes over time. Regular allergy testing of relevant allergens provides information on if and how patient management can be changed to improve health and quality of life. Annual <a href="/wiki/Allergy_test" title="Allergy test">testing</a> is often the practice for determining whether allergy to milk, egg, soy, and wheat have been outgrown, and the testing interval is extended to 2–3 years for allergy to peanut, tree nuts, fish, and crustacean shellfish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ReferenceB&#95;104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Results of follow-up testing can guide decision-making regarding whether and when it is safe to introduce or re-introduce allergenic food into the diet.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-United&#95;States&#95;2010&#95;107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-United_States_2010-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Skin_prick_testing">Skin prick testing</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Skin prick testing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Allergy_skin_testing.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Allergy_skin_testing.JPG/250px-Allergy_skin_testing.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Allergy_skin_testing.JPG/500px-Allergy_skin_testing.JPG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a><figcaption>Skin testing on arm</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Skintest2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Skintest2.jpg/250px-Skintest2.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="182" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Skintest2.jpg/500px-Skintest2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="565" data-file-height="411" /></a><figcaption>Skin testing on back</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Skin_test" title="Skin test">Skin testing</a> is also known as "puncture testing" and "prick testing" since it involves a series of tiny punctures or pricks being placed into the patient's skin. Tiny amounts of suspected allergens and/or their <a href="/wiki/Extracts" class="mw-redirect" title="Extracts">extracts</a> (<i>e.g.</i>, pollen, grass, mite proteins, peanut extract) are introduced to sites on the skin marked with pen or dye (the ink/dye should be carefully selected, lest it cause an allergic response itself). A negative and positive control are also included for comparison (e.g., negative is saline or glycerin; positive is histamine). A small plastic or metal device is used to puncture or prick the skin. Sometimes, allergens are injected "intradermally" into the patient's skin, with a needle and syringe. Common areas for testing include the inside of the forearm and the back.
</p><p>If the patient is allergic to the substance, then a visible inflammatory reaction will usually occur within 30&#160;minutes. This response ranges from slight reddening of the skin to a full-blown <a href="/wiki/Urticaria" class="mw-redirect" title="Urticaria">hive</a> (called "wheal and flare") in more sensitive patients, similar to a <a href="/wiki/Mosquito_bite" class="mw-redirect" title="Mosquito bite">mosquito bite</a>. Interpretation of the results of the skin prick test is usually done by allergists on a scale of severity, with +/− meaning borderline reactivity, and 4+ representing a large reaction. Increasingly, allergists are measuring and recording the diameter of the wheal and flare reaction. Interpretation by well-trained allergists is often guided by relevant literature.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid16164451&#95;108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16164451-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In general, a positive response is interpreted when the wheal of an antigen is ≥3mm larger than the wheal of the negative control (e.g., saline or glycerin).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some patients may believe they have determined their own allergic sensitivity from observation. A skin test is much better than patient observation for allergy detection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid11101180&#95;110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11101180-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>If a serious life-threatening anaphylactic reaction has brought a patient in for evaluation, some allergists will prefer an initial blood test before performing the skin prick test. Skin tests may not be an option if the patient has widespread skin disease or has taken <a href="/wiki/Antihistamines" class="mw-redirect" title="Antihistamines">antihistamines</a> in the last several days.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Patch_testing">Patch testing</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Patch testing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Patch_test" title="Patch test">Patch test</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Epikutanni-test.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Epikutanni-test.jpg/250px-Epikutanni-test.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Epikutanni-test.jpg/500px-Epikutanni-test.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption>Patch test</figcaption></figure>
<p>Patch testing is a method used to determine whether a specific substance causes allergic skin inflammation. It tests for delayed reactions. It is used to help ascertain the cause of skin contact allergy or <a href="/wiki/Contact_dermatitis" title="Contact dermatitis">contact dermatitis</a>. Adhesive patches, usually treated with several common allergic chemicals or skin sensitizers, are applied to the back. The skin is then examined for possible local reactions at least twice, usually at 48 hours after patch application, and again two or three days later.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Blood_testing">Blood testing</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Blood testing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An allergy <a href="/wiki/Blood_test" title="Blood test">blood test</a> is quick and simple and can be ordered by a licensed health care provider (<i>e.g.</i>, an allergy specialist) or general practitioner. Unlike skin-prick testing, a blood test can be performed irrespective of age, skin condition, medication, symptom, disease activity, and pregnancy. Adults and children of any age can get an allergy blood test. For babies and very young children, a single needle stick for allergy blood testing is often gentler than several skin pricks.
</p><p>An allergy blood test is available through most <a href="/wiki/Medical_laboratory" title="Medical laboratory">laboratories</a>. A sample of the patient's blood is sent to a laboratory for analysis, and the results are sent back a few days later. Multiple allergens can be detected with a single blood sample. Allergy blood tests are very safe since the person is not exposed to any allergens during the testing procedure. After the onset of anaphylaxis or a severe allergic reaction, guidelines recommend that emergency departments obtain a time-sensitive blood test to determine blood tryptase levels and assess for mast cell activation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The test measures the concentration of specific <a href="/wiki/IgE" class="mw-redirect" title="IgE">IgE antibodies</a> in the blood. <a href="/wiki/Quantitative_analysis_(chemistry)" title="Quantitative analysis (chemistry)">Quantitative</a> IgE test results increase the possibility of ranking how different substances may affect symptoms. A rule of thumb is that the higher the IgE antibody value, the greater the likelihood of symptoms. Allergens found at low levels that today do not result in symptoms cannot predict future symptom development. The quantitative allergy blood result can help determine what a patient is allergic to, help predict and follow the disease development, estimate the risk of a severe reaction, and explain <a href="/wiki/Cross-reactivity" title="Cross-reactivity">cross-reactivity</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A low total IgE level is not adequate to rule out <a href="/wiki/Sensitization_(immunology)" title="Sensitization (immunology)">sensitization</a> to commonly inhaled allergens.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid12911420&#95;114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12911420-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">Statistical methods</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/ROC_curve" class="mw-redirect" title="ROC curve">ROC curves</a>, predictive value calculations, and likelihood ratios have been used to examine the relationship of various testing methods to each other. These methods have shown that patients with high total IgE have a high probability of allergic sensitization, but further investigation with allergy tests for specific IgE antibodies for a carefully chosen set of allergens is often warranted.
</p><p>Laboratory methods to measure specific IgE antibodies for allergy testing include <a href="/wiki/Enzyme-linked_immunosorbent_assay" class="mw-redirect" title="Enzyme-linked immunosorbent assay">enzyme-linked immunosorbent assay</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-webmd&#95;115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webmd-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Radioallergosorbent_test" title="Radioallergosorbent test">radioallergosorbent test</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-webmd&#95;115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-webmd-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> fluorescent enzyme <a href="/wiki/Immunoassay" title="Immunoassay">immunoassay</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-KhanUeno-Yamanouchi2012&#95;116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KhanUeno-Yamanouchi2012-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and chemiluminescence immunoassay.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other_testing">Other testing</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other testing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:MMT_muscle.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/MMT_muscle.jpg/250px-MMT_muscle.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="375" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/MMT_muscle.jpg/500px-MMT_muscle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="700" data-file-height="1050" /></a><figcaption>An applied kinesiologist demonstrating a manual muscle test (MMT), which is ineffective in cases of allergies.</figcaption></figure>
<p><b>Challenge testing:</b> Challenge testing is when tiny amounts of a suspected allergen are introduced to the body orally, through inhalation, or via other routes. Except for testing food and medication allergies, challenges are rarely performed. When this type of testing is chosen, it must be closely supervised by an <a href="/wiki/Allergist" title="Allergist">allergist</a>.
</p><p><b>Elimination/challenge tests:</b> This testing method is used most often with foods or medicines. A patient with a suspected allergen is instructed to avoid that allergen entirely for a set time. If the patient experiences significant improvement, he may then be "challenged" by reintroducing the allergen to see whether symptoms recur.
</p><p><b>Unreliable tests:</b> There are other types of allergy testing methods that are unreliable, including <a href="/wiki/Applied_kinesiology" title="Applied kinesiology">applied kinesiology</a> (allergy testing through muscle relaxation), <a href="/wiki/Cytotoxicity" title="Cytotoxicity">cytotoxicity</a> testing, urine autoinjection, skin <a href="/wiki/Titration" title="Titration">titration</a> (Rinkel method), and provocative and neutralization (subcutaneous) testing or sublingual provocation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Allergy&#95;Diagnosis&#95;119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allergy_Diagnosis-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Differential_diagnosis">Differential diagnosis</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Differential diagnosis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Before a diagnosis of allergic disease can be confirmed, other plausible causes of the presenting symptoms must be considered.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vasomotor_rhinitis" class="mw-redirect" title="Vasomotor rhinitis">Vasomotor rhinitis</a>, for example, is one of many illnesses that share symptoms with allergic rhinitis, underscoring the need for professional differential diagnosis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid16190503&#95;121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16190503-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Once a diagnosis of <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>, rhinitis, anaphylaxis, or other allergic disease has been made, there are several methods for discovering the causative agent of that allergy.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Prevention">Prevention</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Prevention"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Peanut_Snack_(26238488870).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Peanut_Snack_%2826238488870%29.jpg/250px-Peanut_Snack_%2826238488870%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="195" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Peanut_Snack_%2826238488870%29.jpg/500px-Peanut_Snack_%2826238488870%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3120" data-file-height="2436" /></a><figcaption>Researchers designed the Learning Early About <a href="/wiki/Peanut_Allergy" class="mw-redirect" title="Peanut Allergy">Peanut Allergy</a> (LEAP) study after noting that Israeli children, who start eating peanut foods early, have lower peanut allergy rates than similar children in the UK; infants in the study were fed a peanut snack pictured in the image.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Allergy_prevention_in_children" class="mw-redirect" title="Allergy prevention in children">Allergy prevention in children</a></div>
<p>Giving peanut products early in childhood may decrease the risk of allergies, and only <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">breastfeeding</a> during at least the first few months of life may decrease the risk of allergic <a href="/wiki/Dermatitis" title="Dermatitis">dermatitis</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gre2019&#95;123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gre2019-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gar2018&#95;124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gar2018-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is little evidence that a mother's diet during pregnancy or breastfeeding affects the risk of allergies,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gre2019&#95;123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gre2019-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> although there has been some research to show that irregular cow's milk exposure might increase the risk of cow's milk allergy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is some evidence that delayed introduction of certain foods is useful,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gre2019&#95;123-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gre2019-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and that early exposure to potential allergens may actually be protective.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sic2014&#95;5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sic2014-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to recent studies from pediatric professionals in the U.K. and North Carolina, they suggest that early exposure to peanut products has greatly reduced <a href="/wiki/Peanut_allergies" class="mw-redirect" title="Peanut allergies">peanut allergies</a> among young children in the U.S. Following milestone research from a decade ago, the studies found that pediatric guidelines from 2015 and 2017 were revised to encourage introducing peanuts to high risk infants from the early ages of 4 to 6 months. Researchers who examined the collected data found a 43% drop in the frequency of peanut allergies. This prevented an estimated 40,000 cases and resulted in a noticeable decline in overall food allergy cases. While this is notable, some pediatric experts want to ensure that caution is still provided to the public about the real-world implementation of guidelines since there are potential inconsistencies and limitations. These findings focus on the benefits and risks of early allergen introductions to hopefully slow the rise of food allergies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Fish oil supplementation during pregnancy is associated with a lower risk of food sensitivities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gar2018&#95;124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gar2018-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Probiotic supplements during pregnancy or infancy may help to prevent atopic dermatitis.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Management">Management</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Management"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Management of allergies typically involves avoiding the allergy trigger and taking medications to relieve symptoms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Imm&#95;6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Allergen_immunotherapy" title="Allergen immunotherapy">Allergen immunotherapy</a> may be useful for some types of allergies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Imm&#95;6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Medication">Medication</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Medication"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Several medications may be used to block the actions of allergic mediators, prevent cell activation, and <a href="/wiki/Degranulation" title="Degranulation">degranulation</a> processes. These include <a href="/wiki/Antihistamine" title="Antihistamine">antihistamines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glucocorticoid" title="Glucocorticoid">glucocorticoids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine_(medication)" title="Epinephrine (medication)">epinephrine</a> (adrenaline), <a href="/wiki/Mast_cell_stabilizer" title="Mast cell stabilizer">mast cell stabilizers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antileukotriene_agent" class="mw-redirect" title="Antileukotriene agent">antileukotriene agents</a> are common treatments of allergic diseases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-MCAS&#95;129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MCAS-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anticholinergic" title="Anticholinergic">Anticholinergics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Decongestant" title="Decongestant">decongestants</a>, and other compounds thought to impair <a href="/wiki/Eosinophil" title="Eosinophil">eosinophil</a> <a href="/wiki/Chemotaxis" title="Chemotaxis">chemotaxis</a> are also commonly used. Although rare, the severity of anaphylaxis often requires <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine_(medication)" title="Epinephrine (medication)">epinephrine</a> injection, and where medical care is unavailable, a device known as an <a href="/wiki/Epinephrine_autoinjector" title="Epinephrine autoinjector">epinephrine autoinjector</a> may be used.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tang03&#95;34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tang03-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Immunotherapy">Immunotherapy</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Immunotherapy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Allergen_immunotherapy" title="Allergen immunotherapy">Allergen immunotherapy</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Anti-Allergy_Immunotherapy.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Anti-Allergy_Immunotherapy.jpg/250px-Anti-Allergy_Immunotherapy.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="186" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Anti-Allergy_Immunotherapy.jpg/500px-Anti-Allergy_Immunotherapy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2083" data-file-height="1552" /></a><figcaption>Anti-allergy immunotherapy</figcaption></figure>
<p>Allergen <a href="/wiki/Immunotherapy" title="Immunotherapy">immunotherapy</a> is useful for environmental allergies, allergies to insect bites, and asthma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Imm&#95;6-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Abra2010&#95;130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abra2010-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Its benefit for food allergies is unclear and thus not recommended.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Imm&#95;6-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Immunotherapy involves exposing people to larger and larger amounts of allergen in an effort to change the immune system's response.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NIH2015Imm&#95;6-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2015Imm-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Meta-analyses have found that injections of allergens under the skin is effective in the treatment in allergic rhinitis in children<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Penagos06&#95;131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Penagos06-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in asthma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Abra2010&#95;130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abra2010-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The benefits may last for years after treatment is stopped.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Canonica09&#95;133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canonica09-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is generally safe and effective for allergic rhinitis and <a href="/wiki/Allergic_conjunctivitis" title="Allergic conjunctivitis">conjunctivitis</a>, allergic forms of asthma, and stinging insects.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid17803880&#95;134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17803880-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>To a lesser extent, the evidence also supports the use of <a href="/wiki/Sublingual_immunotherapy" class="mw-redirect" title="Sublingual immunotherapy">sublingual immunotherapy</a> for rhinitis and asthma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Canonica09&#95;133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canonica09-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For seasonal allergies the benefit is small.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In this form the allergen is given under the tongue and people often prefer it to injections.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Canonica09&#95;133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canonica09-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Immunotherapy is not recommended as a stand-alone treatment for asthma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Canonica09&#95;133-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canonica09-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Petasites_hybridus_inflorescence_-_Keila.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Petasites_hybridus_inflorescence_-_Keila.jpg/250px-Petasites_hybridus_inflorescence_-_Keila.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="300" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Petasites_hybridus_inflorescence_-_Keila.jpg/500px-Petasites_hybridus_inflorescence_-_Keila.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4000" data-file-height="6000" /></a><figcaption>Flower of a butterbur</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Alternative_medicine">Alternative medicine</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Alternative medicine"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An experimental treatment, <a href="/wiki/Enzyme_potentiated_desensitization" title="Enzyme potentiated desensitization">enzyme potentiated desensitization</a> (EPD), has been tried for decades but is not generally accepted as effective.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid15042943&#95;136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15042943-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> EPD uses dilutions of allergen and an enzyme, <a href="/wiki/Beta-glucuronidase" class="mw-redirect" title="Beta-glucuronidase">beta-glucuronidase</a>, to which <a href="/wiki/T-lymphocytes,_regulatory" class="mw-redirect" title="T-lymphocytes, regulatory">T-regulatory lymphocytes</a> are supposed to respond by favoring desensitization, or down-regulation, rather than sensitization. EPD has also been tried for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Autoimmune_diseases" class="mw-redirect" title="Autoimmune diseases">autoimmune diseases</a>, but evidence does not show effectiveness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid15042943&#95;136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15042943-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A review found no effectiveness of <a href="/wiki/Homeopathic_treatment" class="mw-redirect" title="Homeopathic treatment">homeopathic treatments</a> and no difference compared with <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebo</a>. The authors concluded that, based on rigorous clinical trials of all types of homeopathy for childhood and adolescent ailments, there is no convincing evidence that supports the use of homeopathic treatments.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid17285788&#95;137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17285788-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Complementary_and_Integrative_Health" title="National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health">National Center for Complementary and Integrative Health</a>, U.S., the evidence is relatively strong that <a href="/wiki/Saline_nasal_irrigation" class="mw-redirect" title="Saline nasal irrigation">saline nasal irrigation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Butterbur" class="mw-redirect" title="Butterbur">butterbur</a> are effective, when compared to other <a href="/wiki/Alternative_medicine" title="Alternative medicine">alternative medicine</a> treatments, for which the scientific evidence is weak, negative, or nonexistent, such as honey, acupuncture, omega 3's, probiotics, astragalus, capsaicin, grape seed extract, Pycnogenol, quercetin, spirulina, stinging nettle, tinospora, or guduchi.
<sup id="cite&#95;ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Epidemiology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The allergic diseases—hay fever and asthma—have increased in the Western world over the past 2–3 decades.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Platts&#95;140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Platts-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Increases in allergic asthma and other atopic disorders in industrialized nations, it is estimated, began in the 1960s and 1970s, with further increases occurring during the 1980s and 1990s,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bloomfield&#95;141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomfield-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> although some suggest that a steady rise in sensitization has been occurring since the 1920s.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-The&#95;allergy&#95;epidemic&#95;extends&#95;beyond&#95;the&#95;past&#95;few&#95;decades&#95;142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_allergy_epidemic_extends_beyond_the_past_few_decades-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The number of new cases per year of <a href="/wiki/Atopy" title="Atopy">atopy</a> in developing countries has, in general, remained much lower.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bloomfield&#95;141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomfield-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="width:70%; float:center; font-size:90%; margin-left:15px">

<td>10&#160;million have allergic asthma (about 3% of the population). The prevalence of asthma increased 75% from 1980 to 1994. <td>5.7&#160;million (about 9.4%). In six- and seven-year-olds, asthma increased from 18.4% to 20.9% over five years; during the same time, the rate decreased from 31% to 24.7% in 13- to 14-year-olds.
</td></tr>

<td>Atopic eczema</td>
<td>About 9% of the population. Between 1960 and 1990, the prevalence increased from 3% to 10% in children.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AAAAI&#95;-&#95;skin&#95;condition,&#95;itchy&#95;skin,&#95;bumps,&#95;red&#95;irritated&#95;skin,&#95;allergic&#95;reaction,&#95;treating&#95;skin&#95;condition&#95;148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAAAI_-_skin_condition,_itchy_skin,_bumps,_red_irritated_skin,_allergic_reaction,_treating_skin_condition-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td>

</td></tr>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Changing_frequency">Changing frequency</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Changing frequency"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:F-diagram-01.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/F-diagram-01.jpg/330px-F-diagram-01.jpg" decoding="async" width="330" height="130" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/F-diagram-01.jpg/960px-F-diagram-01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6600" data-file-height="2608" /></a><figcaption>The "F-diagram" (<a href="/wiki/Human_feces" title="Human feces">feces</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fingers" class="mw-redirect" title="Fingers">fingers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flies" class="mw-redirect" title="Flies">flies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Field_(agriculture)" title="Field (agriculture)">fields</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fluids" class="mw-redirect" title="Fluids">fluids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a>), showing pathways of fecal–oral disease transmission. The vertical blue lines show barriers: <a href="/wiki/Toilet" title="Toilet">toilets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">safe water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hygiene" title="Hygiene">hygiene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Handwashing" class="mw-redirect" title="Handwashing">handwashing</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Although genetic factors govern susceptibility to atopic disease, increases in <a href="/wiki/Atopy" title="Atopy">atopy</a> have occurred within too short a period to be explained by a genetic change in the population, thus pointing to environmental or lifestyle changes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bloomfield&#95;141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomfield-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Several hypotheses have been identified to explain this increased rate. Increased exposure to perennial allergens may be due to housing changes and increased time spent indoors, and changes in cleanliness may decrease activation of a common immune control mechanism.s<sup id="cite&#95;ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or hygiene, and exacerbated by dietary changes, obesity, and decline in physical exercise.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Platts&#95;140-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Platts-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hygiene_hypothesis" title="Hygiene hypothesis">hygiene hypothesis</a> maintains<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hay&#95;fever,&#95;hygiene,&#95;and&#95;household&#95;size&#95;155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hay_fever,_hygiene,_and_household_size-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> that high living standards and hygienic conditions expose children to fewer infections. It is thought that reduced bacterial and viral infections early in life direct the maturing immune system away from <a href="/wiki/T_helper_cell" class="mw-redirect" title="T helper cell">T<sub>H</sub></a>1 type responses, leading to unrestrained T<sub>H</sub>2 responses that allow for an increase in allergy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Yazdanbakhsh02&#95;95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yazdanbakhsh02-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Renz&#95;156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Renz-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Changes in rates and types of infection alone, however, have been unable to explain the observed increase in allergic disease, and recent evidence has focused attention on the importance of the <a href="/wiki/Gut_flora" class="mw-redirect" title="Gut flora">gastrointestinal microbial environment</a>. Evidence has shown that exposure to food and <a href="/wiki/Fecal-oral_route" class="mw-redirect" title="Fecal-oral route">fecal-oral</a> pathogens, such as <a href="/wiki/Hepatitis_A" title="Hepatitis A">hepatitis A</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Toxoplasma_gondii" title="Toxoplasma gondii">Toxoplasma gondii</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Helicobacter_pylori" title="Helicobacter pylori">Helicobacter pylori</a></i> (which also tend to be more prevalent in developing countries), can reduce the overall risk of atopy by more than 60%,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Exposure&#95;to&#95;foodborne&#95;and&#95;orofecal&#95;microbes&#95;versus&#95;airborne&#95;viruses&#95;in&#95;relation&#95;to&#95;atopy&#95;and&#95;allergic&#95;asthma:&#95;epidemiological&#95;study&#95;157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Exposure_to_foodborne_and_orofecal_microbes_versus_airborne_viruses_in_relation_to_atopy_and_allergic_asthma:_epidemiological_study-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and an increased rate of parasitic infections has been associated with a decreased prevalence of asthma.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Parasites&#95;and&#95;asthma--predictive&#95;or&#95;protective?&#95;158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parasites_and_asthma--predictive_or_protective?-158"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is speculated that these infections exert their effect by critically altering T<sub>H</sub>1/T<sub>H</sub>2 regulation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sheikh&#95;159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheikh-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Important elements of newer hygiene hypotheses also include exposure to <a href="/wiki/Endotoxin" class="mw-redirect" title="Endotoxin">endotoxins</a>, exposure to pets and growing up on a farm.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sheikh&#95;159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheikh-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Cameo_of_France_CdM_Paris_Bab264_n1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Great_Cameo_of_France_CdM_Paris_Bab264_n1.jpg/250px-Great_Cameo_of_France_CdM_Paris_Bab264_n1.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="270" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Great_Cameo_of_France_CdM_Paris_Bab264_n1.jpg/500px-Great_Cameo_of_France_CdM_Paris_Bab264_n1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2450" data-file-height="2650" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Cameo_of_France" title="Great Cameo of France">Great Cameo of France</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Cameo_(carving)" title="Cameo (carving)">cameo</a> five layers <a href="/wiki/Sardonyx" class="mw-redirect" title="Sardonyx">sardonyx</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, c. AD 23, depicting the emperor <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> seated with his mother <a href="/wiki/Livia" title="Livia">Livia</a> and in front of his designated heir <a href="/wiki/Germanicus" title="Germanicus">Germanicus</a>, with the latter's wife <a href="/wiki/Agrippina_the_Elder" title="Agrippina the Elder">Agrippina the Elder</a>; above them float the deceased members of their house: <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Drusus_Julius_Caesar" title="Drusus Julius Caesar">Drusus Julius Caesar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nero_Claudius_Drusus" title="Nero Claudius Drusus">Nero Claudius Drusus</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Some symptoms attributable to allergic diseases are mentioned in ancient sources.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-aai&#95;160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aai-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Particularly, three members of the Roman <a href="/wiki/Julio-Claudian_dynasty" title="Julio-Claudian dynasty">Julio-Claudian dynasty</a> (<a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Claudius" title="Claudius">Claudius</a> and <a href="/wiki/Britannicus" title="Britannicus">Britannicus</a>) are suspected to have a family history of <a href="/wiki/Atopy" title="Atopy">atopy</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-aai&#95;160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aai-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>161<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The concept of "allergy" was originally introduced in 1906 by the <a href="/wiki/Vienna,_Austria" class="mw-redirect" title="Vienna, Austria">Viennese</a> <a href="/wiki/Pediatrician" class="mw-redirect" title="Pediatrician">pediatrician</a> <a href="/wiki/Clemens_von_Pirquet" title="Clemens von Pirquet">Clemens von Pirquet</a>, after he noticed that patients who had received injections of horse serum or smallpox vaccine usually had quicker, more severe reactions to second injections.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>162<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Pirquet called this phenomenon "allergy" from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a> words <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%84%CE%BB%CE%BB%CE%BF%CF%82" class="extiw" title="wikt:ἄλλος">ἄλλος</a> <i>allos</i> meaning "other" and <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%94%CF%81%CE%B3%CE%BF%CE%BD" class="extiw" title="wikt:ἔργον">ἔργον</a> <i>ergon</i> meaning "work".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Allergie&#95;163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allergie-163"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>163<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>All forms of hypersensitivity were previously classified as allergies, and all were thought to be caused by an improper activation of the immune system. Later, it became clear that several disease mechanisms were implicated, with a common link to disordered immune system activation. In 1963, a new classification scheme was designed by <a href="/wiki/Philip_George_Houthem_Gell" title="Philip George Houthem Gell">Philip Gell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robin_Coombs" title="Robin Coombs">Robin Coombs</a> that described four types of <a href="/wiki/Hypersensitivities" class="mw-redirect" title="Hypersensitivities">hypersensitivity reactions</a>, known as Type I to Type IV hypersensitivity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-GellCoombs&#95;164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GellCoombs-164"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>164<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>With this new classification, the word <i>allergy</i>, sometimes clarified as a <i>true allergy</i>, was restricted to type I hypersensitivities (also called immediate hypersensitivity), which are characterized as rapidly developing reactions involving IgE antibodies.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>165<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A breakthrough in understanding the mechanisms of allergy was the discovery of the antibody class labeled <a href="/wiki/Immunoglobulin_E" title="Immunoglobulin E">immunoglobulin E</a> (IgE). IgE was simultaneously discovered in 1966–67 by two independent groups:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>166<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kimishige_Ishizaka" title="Kimishige Ishizaka">Ishizaka</a>'s team at the Children's Asthma Research Institute and Hospital in Denver, USA,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ishizaka&#95;K&#95;167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ishizaka_K-167"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>167<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and by <a href="/wiki/Gunnar_Johansson_(immunologist)" title="Gunnar Johansson (immunologist)">Gunnar Johansson</a> and Hans Bennich in Uppsala, Sweden.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>168<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Their joint paper was published in April 1969.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Joint&#95;paper&#95;1969&#95;169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Joint_paper_1969-169"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>169<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Diagnosis_2">Diagnosis</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Diagnosis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Radiometric assays include the <a href="/wiki/Radioallergosorbent_test" title="Radioallergosorbent test">radioallergosorbent test</a> (RAST test) method, which uses IgE-binding (anti-IgE) antibodies labeled with <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_isotope" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive isotope">radioactive isotopes</a> for quantifying the levels of IgE antibody in the blood.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid7630219&#95;170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid7630219-170"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>170<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The RAST methodology was invented and marketed in 1974 by Pharmacia Diagnostics AB, Uppsala, Sweden, and the acronym RAST is actually a brand name. In 1989, Pharmacia Diagnostics AB replaced it with a superior test named the ImmunoCAP Specific IgE blood test, which uses the newer fluorescence-labeled technology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-n663&#95;171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-n663-171"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>171<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/American_College_of_Allergy,_Asthma_and_Immunology" title="American College of Allergy, Asthma and Immunology">American College of Allergy Asthma and Immunology</a> (ACAAI) and the <a href="/wiki/American_Academy_of_Allergy,_Asthma,_and_Immunology" title="American Academy of Allergy, Asthma, and Immunology">American Academy of Allergy Asthma and Immunology</a> (AAAAI) issued the Joint Task Force Report "Pearls and pitfalls of allergy diagnostic testing" in 2008, and is firm in its statement that the term RAST is now obsolete:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The term RAST became a colloquialism for all varieties of (in vitro allergy) tests.  This is unfortunate because it is well recognized that there are well-performing tests and some that do not perform as well, yet they are all called RASTs, making it difficult to distinguish between them. For these reasons, it is now recommended that the use of RAST as a generic descriptor of these tests be abandoned.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cox2008&#95;17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cox2008-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The updated version, the ImmunoCAP Specific IgE blood test, is the only specific IgE assay the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Drug_Administration" title="Food and Drug Administration">Food and Drug Administration</a> has approved to quantitatively report to its detection limit of 0.1kU/L.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-n663&#95;171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-n663-171"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>171<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Medical_specialty">Medical specialty</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Medical specialty"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1316064257" /><table class="infobox"><caption class="infobox-title">Allergist/Immunologist</caption><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header">Occupation</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Names</th><td class="infobox-data">
<ul><li>Physician</li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Occupation type</div></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Specialty_(medicine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Specialty (medicine)">Specialty</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Activity sectors</div></th><td class="infobox-data">Medicine</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Specialty</th><td class="infobox-data"><span><span><a href="/wiki/Immunology" title="Immunology">immunology</a></span></span></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header">Description</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Education required</div></th><td class="infobox-data">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Medicine" title="Doctor of Medicine">Doctor of Medicine</a> (M.D.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Osteopathic_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Doctor of Osteopathic medicine">Doctor of Osteopathic medicine</a> (D.O.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Medicine,_Bachelor_of_Surgery" title="Bachelor of Medicine, Bachelor of Surgery">Bachelor of Medicine, Bachelor of Surgery</a> (M.B.B.S.)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_Medicine,_Bachelor_of_Surgery" title="Bachelor of Medicine, Bachelor of Surgery">Bachelor of Medicine, Bachelor of Surgery</a> (MBChB)</li></ul>
</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Fields of<br />employment</div></th><td class="infobox-data">Hospitals, Clinics</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The medical speciality that studies, diagnoses, and treats diseases caused by allergies is called allergology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>172<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
An allergist is a physician specially trained to manage and treat allergies, asthma, and other allergic diseases. In the United States, physicians holding certification by the <a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Board_of_Allergy_and_Immunology&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="American Board of Allergy and Immunology (page does not exist)">American Board of Allergy and Immunology</a> (ABAI) have completed an accredited educational program and evaluation process, including a proctored examination to demonstrate knowledge, skills, and experience in patient care in allergy and immunology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ABAI:&#95;American&#95;Board&#95;of&#95;Allergy&#95;and&#95;Immunology&#95;173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ABAI:_American_Board_of_Allergy_and_Immunology-173"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Becoming an allergist/immunologist requires completion of at least nine years of training.
</p><p>After completing medical school and graduating with a medical degree, a physician will undergo three years of training in <a href="/wiki/Internal_medicine" title="Internal medicine">internal medicine</a> (to become an internist) or <a href="/wiki/Pediatrics" title="Pediatrics">pediatrics</a> (to become a pediatrician). Once physicians have finished training in one of these specialties, they must pass the exam of either the <a href="/wiki/American_Board_of_Pediatrics" title="American Board of Pediatrics">American Board of Pediatrics</a> (ABP), the <a href="/wiki/American_Osteopathic_Board_of_Pediatrics" title="American Osteopathic Board of Pediatrics">American Osteopathic Board of Pediatrics</a> (AOBP), the <a href="/wiki/American_Board_of_Internal_Medicine" title="American Board of Internal Medicine">American Board of Internal Medicine</a> (ABIM), or the <a href="/wiki/American_Osteopathic_Board_of_Internal_Medicine" title="American Osteopathic Board of Internal Medicine">American Osteopathic Board of Internal Medicine</a> (AOBIM). Internists or pediatricians wishing to focus on the sub-specialty of allergy-immunology then complete at least an additional two years of study, called a fellowship, in an allergy/immunology training program. Allergists/immunologists listed as ABAI-certified have successfully passed the certifying ABAI examination following their fellowship.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AAAAI&#95;-&#95;What&#95;is&#95;an&#95;Allergist?&#95;174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAAAI_-_What_is_an_Allergist?-174"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>174<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the United Kingdom, allergy is a subspecialty of <a href="/wiki/General_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="General medicine">general medicine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pediatrics" title="Pediatrics">pediatrics</a>. After obtaining postgraduate exams (<a href="/wiki/Membership_of_the_Royal_College_of_Physicians" class="mw-redirect" title="Membership of the Royal College of Physicians">MRCP</a> or <a href="/w/index.php?title=Membership_of_the_Royal_College_of_Paediatrics_and_Child_Health&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Membership of the Royal College of Paediatrics and Child Health (page does not exist)">MRCPCH</a>), a doctor works for several years as a <a href="/wiki/Specialist_registrar" class="mw-redirect" title="Specialist registrar">specialist registrar</a> before qualifying for the <a href="/wiki/General_Medical_Council" title="General Medical Council">General Medical Council</a> specialist register. Allergy services may also be delivered by <a href="/wiki/Immunologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Immunologist">immunologists</a>. A 2003 <a href="/wiki/Royal_College_of_Physicians" title="Royal College of Physicians">Royal College of Physicians</a> report presented a case for improvement of what were felt to be inadequate allergy services in the UK.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>175<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> convened a subcommittee. It concluded likewise in 2007 that allergy services were insufficient to deal with what the Lords referred to as an "allergy epidemic" and its social cost; it made several recommendations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Allergy&#95;-&#95;HL&#95;166-I,&#95;6th&#95;Report&#95;of&#95;Session&#95;2006-07&#95;-&#95;Volume&#95;1:&#95;Report&#95;176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Allergy_-_HL_166-I,_6th_Report_of_Session_2006-07_-_Volume_1:_Report-176"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>176<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Research">Research</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Allergy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Research"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Low-allergen foods are being developed, as are improvements in skin prick test predictions; evaluation of the atopy patch test, wasp sting outcomes predictions, a rapidly disintegrating epinephrine tablet, and anti-<a href="/wiki/Interleukin_5" title="Interleukin 5">IL-5</a> for eosinophilic diseases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Advances&#95;in&#95;allergic&#95;skin&#95;disease,&#95;anaphylaxis,&#95;and&#95;hypersensitivity&#95;reactions&#95;to&#95;foods,&#95;drugs,&#95;and&#95;insects&#95;177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Advances_in_allergic_skin_disease,_anaphylaxis,_and_hypersensitivity_reactions_to_foods,_drugs,_and_insects-177"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>177<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
